  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({

        scope : 'name email',

<span id="freeTextContainer6579539512944005899"><div>Four years after the Chmielnicki massacres of the seventeenth century, Jacob, a slave and cowherd in a Polish village high in the mountains, falls in love with Wanda, his master's daughter.   <span id="freeText6579539512944005899" style="display:none"><div>Four years after the Chmielnicki massacres of the seventeenth century, Jacob, a slave and cowherd in a Polish village high in the mountains, falls in love with Wanda, his master's daughter. Even after he is ransomed, he finds he can't live without her, and the two escape together to a distant Jewish community. Racked by his consciousness of sin in taking a Gentile wife and by the difficulties of concealing her identity, Jacob nonetheless stands firm as the violence of the era threatens to destroy the ill-fated couple. <br /></div></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1236001691" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2767656399802344192"><br/><br/>Translated from the Yiddish by the author and Cecil Hemley <br/><br/>Description: <i>Four years after the Chmielnicki massacres of the seventeenth century, Jacob, a slave and cowherd in a Polish village high in the mountains, falls in love with Wanda, his master's daughter. Even after he is ransomed, he finds he can't live without her, and the two escape together to a distant Jewish community. Racked by his consciousness of sin in taking a Gentile wife and by the difficulties of concealing her identity, Jaco</i></span>
  <span id="freeText2767656399802344192" style="display:none"><br><br>Translated from the Yiddish by the author and Cecil Hemley <br><br>Description: <i>Four years after the Chmielnicki massacres of the seventeenth century, Jacob, a slave and cowherd in a Polish village high in the mountains, falls in love with Wanda, his master's daughter. Even after he is ransomed, he finds he can't live without her, and the two escape together to a distant Jewish community. Racked by his consciousness of sin in taking a Gentile wife and by the difficulties of concealing her identity, Jacob nonetheless stands firm as the violence of the era threatens to destroy the ill-fated couple. </i><br><br>Opening: <b>A single bird call began the day. Each day the same bird, the same call.</b><br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/books/1439985430s/25743.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg">An unusual love story, yet one, thankfully, that doesn't hover on sentimental hogwash. If you love bizarre superstitions including succubi, vampires, Dizwosina and Skrots, then this may just be the book for you. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1452798479i/17745395.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg">Drowning Baba Yaga.<br><br>Thoroughly disconcerted as to why Poland has willingly returned to media censorship after they fought so hard to slough off the yoke, I go searching for answers in past fiction, well, 'pointers' to be more exact. I read Turkish literature for the same reason, why, after Atatürk's splendid ideals, are there Islamists in government there. So many questions to ponder over, so little time.<br><br>Chmielnicki: the Cossack-Polish War 1648-1657: mass atrocities committed by Cossacks against civilian population, especially against the Roman Catholic clergy and the Jews. Relevent to today's world is thisbit snaffled from wiki: <i>it</i> [this war] <i>led to the eventual incorporation of eastern Ukraine into the Tsardom of Russia.</i><br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1452790106i/17743883._SX540_.png" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></span>
  <a data-text-id="2767656399802344192" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2948908508?book_show_action=true">Aug 23, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Gary" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Gary" href="/user/show/51196399-gary">Gary</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2948908508" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13381819952486422782">A rich, poignant, deep, tragic and somber novel of love, faith and spirituality. Four years after the Chmielnicki massacres of 1648 in which tens of thousands of Jews were massacred by Cossack hordes, a pious Jew Jacob, exists as a slave ,captured during the massacres in the mountains of Poland for gentile, peasants. Treated like a beast, he is shown mercy and tenderness by his master's beautiful daughter Wanda, who he falls in love with.<br/>When he returns to how own Jewish town, Jacob cannot forge</span>
  <span id="freeText13381819952486422782" style="display:none">A rich, poignant, deep, tragic and somber novel of love, faith and spirituality. Four years after the Chmielnicki massacres of 1648 in which tens of thousands of Jews were massacred by Cossack hordes, a pious Jew Jacob, exists as a slave ,captured during the massacres in the mountains of Poland for gentile, peasants. Treated like a beast, he is shown mercy and tenderness by his master's beautiful daughter Wanda, who he falls in love with.<br />When he returns to how own Jewish town, Jacob cannot forget Wanda and brings her back to his home as a convert to Judaism. But as conversion was forbidden in that time and place, he cannot reveal her gentile birth and to cover up this, and not let this be revealed by her Polish accent, he must pass her off as a mute. Her name is now Sarah.<br /><br />Despite her great reservoir of kindness, and piety she is ill treated by most of the community. Jacob has to also forebear the capricious cruelty of the local Polish nobleman Adam Pilsidsky. Before Wanda dies in childbirth she reveals that she is of gentile birth. As a result of pressure from the narrow minded hypocrites within the community especially the execrable Gershon, Sarah is given a donkey's burial, and the baby boy she has delivered declared not a part of the community. But he is looked after by a good hearted couple and is fetched by his father who takes him to the Land of Israel. 20 years pass before Jacob returns and a great miracle takes place to show that Sarah's (Wanda) soul was indeed that of a daughter of Israel. Full of spiritual phenomena, as well as the rich superstitions of the time, this novel will excite those interested in such subjects and is a classic and jewel of Jewish literature by a master in that craft.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13381819952486422782" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_429601978" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/429601978" />
    <a title="Yair Ben-Zvi" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3223578-yair-ben-zvi"><img alt="Yair Ben-Zvi" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1588137043p2/3223578.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/429601978?book_show_action=true">Jul 09, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Yair Ben-Zvi" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Yair Ben-Zvi" href="/user/show/3223578-yair-ben-zvi">Yair Ben-Zvi</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3223578-yair-ben-zvi?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer429601978" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10171986959477275893">This is a stunning work. I can't do it justice. But I'll try with what enfeebled abilities I have. But first the whole story I got this book and took too long to read it.<br/><br/>I first became a "serious reader" in my mid to late high school years. I remember it was George Orwell and Anthony Burgess who, first, introduced to me the idea that books could be more than just fodder thrown by unfulfilled public school teachers at a sneering and half conscious student body to keep them busy and adhere to arbi</span>
  <span id="freeText10171986959477275893" style="display:none">This is a stunning work. I can't do it justice. But I'll try with what enfeebled abilities I have. But first the whole story I got this book and took too long to read it.<br /><br />I first became a "serious reader" in my mid to late high school years. I remember it was George Orwell and Anthony Burgess who, first, introduced to me the idea that books could be more than just fodder thrown by unfulfilled public school teachers at a sneering and half conscious student body to keep them busy and adhere to arbitrary academic standards. Orwell's "Animal Farm" and "1984" as well as Burgess' "A Clockwork Orange" were the first novels to evidence the ability of literature to cut deep and excite, to teach and to terrify, and, most profoundly, entertain and revivify. <br /><br />From there I slowly made my winding way through the annals of many books. Not as many as I'd like, not as many as some friends (both current and former) but many enough to whittle away hours, months, days, years. And amid all that I took slow steps into the realm of Jewish Fiction. There was no direction, no guiding ethos, I just read what I could find and what was recommended to me. Philip Roth was a perverted and neurotic maestro of prose, Saul Bellow was a bloviating dilettante not without his share of genius, Kafka was the patron saint of Jewish misery and 'the guilt without source'. Of course Norman Mailer was the robber baron of arrogance and unparalleled literary ambition. And then the Jews of European and Israeli origins. The list goes on. But with all of that the name Isaac Bashevis Singer popped up in random spots, a soft whisper resonant of a Jewish life and mentality that had all but ceased to be. This need be evidenced no more than by his choice (and superb ability) to write in Yiddish. Not English, not German, not Hebrew, but Yiddish; a writer of the old language, emblematic of diaspora and all that came with it. And that he was awarded the nobel prize of literature and a Jew of orthodoxy (and a vegetarian to boot!) only added to his mystique and appeal.<br /><br />So I read Singer's "Gimpel the Fool" and enjoyed it. Much later I read "The Penitent" which, while skillfully written, was unfortunately brought down by the noxious pedantry and sententious elitism of its protagonist. But all along his novel "The Slave" remained on the backburner. A more or less self-published novelist, an American Jew living in Israel who become very religious and conservative, derided the novel as well written but borderline pornographic. Having been at the stage where I was just simply angry, I bought the book almost as an act of spite against a man I'd never met but who seemed to embody the worst of my then current neuroses and shortcomings, and dreams and aspirations as well. But years have gone by and my obsessions and weaknesses have shifted. And still "The Slave" remained on my perpetual 'to read' pile.<br /><br />When I finally sat down to read the book I consumed it quickly and deeply. The story of Jacob the Polish Jew who is sold into slavery after a horrific pogrom that leaves most of his family dead and his village destroyed, is told in bitter but never embittered terms. It's a superb recitation of a black historical incident. Jacob is exiled to the Polish mountains where he barely ekes out an existence among people who, at best, distrust him, and, at worst, want him dead for the crime of living. But still Jacob maintains, to the best of his ability, his religious Jewish life. This would have been and could have been a monastic tale of personal strength in a time of diaspora but Singer introduces the love interest: the non-Jewish Polish woman Wanda, who proves to be the impetus for Jacob's transformation into one the most tragic, beautiful, and fully realized protagonists in world Jewish literature.<br /><br />Singer pulls no punches here. The non-Jewish Poles are depicted in various states of savagery and depredation, as well as with spots of humanity belying their downtrodden circumstances. Jacob's fellow Jews are trenchantly delineated with a similarly even hand, showing both the beauty and the hypocrisy inherent within the Jewish religious lifestyle under the aegis of ambivalent (at times) and evil (at times) host culture. The true characters in this work are Jacob and Wanda (later Sarah) who, through their doomed love and passionate dedication to each other, create a tableau of such meaning and profundity that's hard to believe this book isn't at the top of more 'best of' lists.<br /><br />Jacob and Wanda, for me, represent the ethereal and complex, the damning and revelatory aspects of love better than anything I've yet read. They aren't meant to be. And they will be torn from each other and destroyed. But like a postlapsarian Adam and Eve, theirs is a love of creation amid a dark world of Lilith and Satan, a fire to be snuffed but to shine brilliantly while it can.<br /><br />This is a sad novel. But it possesses a nobility and an elan in form I've never seen before. It's human. It's love made real in all its maddening perplexities and hypocrisies. Read this if you want a taste of what it is to love, be damned by that love, but to love anyway, because it's human, and the joy is the misery is the joy.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10171986959477275893" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_149414274" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/149414274" />
    <a title="Ana Ruiz" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4991383-ana-ruiz"><img alt="Ana Ruiz" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1461516690p2/4991383.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/149414274?book_show_action=true">Feb 20, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ana Ruiz" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ana Ruiz" href="/user/show/4991383-ana-ruiz">Ana Ruiz</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer149414274" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3165512453949802189"> 	 I realize that this is a love story. What I found charming about this particular love story is that I couldn't understand why it was called one. That is, until the end. The end is beautiful. I've read so many endings; endings that end with a sigh, with a cry, or with a laugh. But this one was perfect. I must admit I was sleepy when I read that last sentence, but I don't think I could've reacted to the finish of this book anyways. It was perfect.<br/><br/>Like I said, for me, this was mostly a ...moreI </span>
  <span id="freeText3165512453949802189" style="display:none"> 	 I realize that this is a love story. What I found charming about this particular love story is that I couldn't understand why it was called one. That is, until the end. The end is beautiful. I've read so many endings; endings that end with a sigh, with a cry, or with a laugh. But this one was perfect. I must admit I was sleepy when I read that last sentence, but I don't think I could've reacted to the finish of this book anyways. It was perfect.<br /><br />Like I said, for me, this was mostly a ...moreI realize that this is a love story. What I found charming about this particular love story is that I couldn't understand why it was called one. That is, until the end. The end is beautiful. I've read so many endings; endings that end with a sigh, with a cry, or with a laugh. But this one was perfect. I must admit I was sleepy when I read that last sentence, but I don't think I could've reacted to the finish of this book anyways. It was perfect.<br /><br />Like I said, for me, this was mostly a story on humanity. On how complex and hideous humanity can be, but how sometimes God and good shine through and how misunderstood that can be. Jacob never thought of himself as good; he lived with the assurance that he would go to hell because he had married Wanda, or Sarah, and thus he was resigned. Having determined that he would go to hell, he could have been as evil and corrupt as he wanted. But he was so good, so perfectly good that it becomes unbelievable, laughably novel-like. (This is, by the way, the only fault I can find in the book.) And the beauty of the main character's disposition gives the whole tale so much bitter-sweetness. The love story is opaqued.<br /><br />Also, Isaac Bashevis Singer is a genius. This is the first novel of his I have read; I had read short stories before, such as "In my Father's Court." I don't know if it's the brilliance of the translation, but I think that there were some passages that one truly had to re-read, because the language used was heart-wrenching, the landscapes painted fairy-like. How rare and beautiful that is.<br /><br />All in all, this was a book that, although smeared with tragedy, was beautiful. I think I may have to reread it. </span>
  <a data-text-id="3165512453949802189" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1793850727" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1793850727" />
    <a title="Judy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3333943-judy"><img alt="Judy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1267037102p2/3333943.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1793850727?book_show_action=true">Oct 28, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Judy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Judy" href="/user/show/3333943-judy">Judy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3333943-judy?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3333943-judy?shelf=literature-in-translation">literature-in-translation</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3333943-judy?shelf=20th-century-fiction">20th-century-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3333943-judy?shelf=nobel-prize-author">nobel-prize-author</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3333943-judy?shelf=books-from-1962">books-from-1962</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1793850727" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5842779866698527052"> I have not yet read anything by I B Singer I did not love. <i>The Slave</i> is no exception. Singer was awarded the Nobel Prize for Literature in 1978 and always wrote in Yiddish until his death in 1991, in Surfside, FL.<br/><br/>After all my reading this year about slavery in America, I come to this reminder that slavery is as old an institution as prostitution. Both seem to be inherent in the human story. <br/><br/><i>The Slave</i> is an epic in 311 pages. Jacob was a learned and pious Jew, son of wealthy parents, who found h</span>
  <span id="freeText5842779866698527052" style="display:none"> I have not yet read anything by I B Singer I did not love. <i>The Slave</i> is no exception. Singer was awarded the Nobel Prize for Literature in 1978 and always wrote in Yiddish until his death in 1991, in Surfside, FL.<br /><br />After all my reading this year about slavery in America, I come to this reminder that slavery is as old an institution as prostitution. Both seem to be inherent in the human story. <br /><br /><i>The Slave</i> is an epic in 311 pages. Jacob was a learned and pious Jew, son of wealthy parents, who found himself a slave to a farmer in a remote mountain village. His birthplace, Josefov, was a Polish town that lay in the path of Ukrainian Cossacks in the 17th century. The ensuing massacres had cleared the town of Jews. Jacob fled, thinking his parents, wife and children dead, then fell into the hands of robbers who sold him into slavery. <br /><br />Though he desperately strove to stay true to his faith, Jacob began to love the farmer's daughter. Wanda was a step above her environment, a practically prehistoric milieu of pagan superstition, tooth and claw existence, and rural poverty. But she was a Gentile and therefore forbidden. Her passion for him finally overcame his religious scruples and they planned to escape.<br /><br />Of course, that plan fell through on the first attempt. Jacob's life from then on is one of perils and his search for redemption, taking him all the way to Israel as part of the early Zionist movement, at last reuniting with Wanda, and on to his final days where he finds peace and wisdom.<br /><br />Besides being a beautiful love story, the novel is also a contemplation of the place of religion in human society including the contradiction that it condemns believers who do not follow its commandments while it honors the phenomenon that spirituality can lift us above our animal nature. The result is a timeless tale.<br /><br />How interesting that Singer published a novel called The Slave just as the Civil Rights Movement was catching fire in America, his adopted country since 1935. </span>
  <a data-text-id="5842779866698527052" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2503303535" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2503303535" />
    <a title="Jigar Brahmbhatt" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3359025-jigar-brahmbhatt"><img alt="Jigar Brahmbhatt" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1604042551p2/3359025.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2503303535?book_show_action=true">Aug 22, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jigar Brahmbhatt" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jigar Brahmbhatt" href="/user/show/3359025-jigar-brahmbhatt">Jigar Brahmbhatt</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2503303535" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2056883875202468335">I am rarely as enamored by a prose as I am with Singer's. In his earthy storytelling, where nothing is spared, where one gets the full arch of a story, populated with everything human experience can hold, one gets the satisfaction of a full course meal, unlike the trimmed down fast food of the po-mo or experimental kind. <br/><br/>We journey with Joseph, a Jew sold in slavery, for decades as his love for Wanda, a gentile, grows into something beyond passion, challenging and get challenged by the epoch, as</span>
  <span id="freeText2056883875202468335" style="display:none">I am rarely as enamored by a prose as I am with Singer's. In his earthy storytelling, where nothing is spared, where one gets the full arch of a story, populated with everything human experience can hold, one gets the satisfaction of a full course meal, unlike the trimmed down fast food of the po-mo or experimental kind. <br /><br />We journey with Joseph, a Jew sold in slavery, for decades as his love for Wanda, a gentile, grows into something beyond passion, challenging and get challenged by the epoch, as we see them form a strange family, always threatened by people and tradition, and the hovering shadow of the attacking Cossacks. This is Poland of centuries past. And we witness it with every changing season. Every creature and every vegetation we encounter has a connection with the larger narrative, and we wonder how an entire culture, a way of the people is preserved in this novel. Where does storytelling this rich come from? Not from technique, but from the belly of a writer who has digested his experiences well.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2056883875202468335" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_698191564" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/698191564" />
    <a title="Kerstin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/969389-kerstin"><img alt="Kerstin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1600041163p2/969389.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">

        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/698191564?book_show_action=true">Aug 18, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kerstin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kerstin" href="/user/show/969389-kerstin">Kerstin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer698191564" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4888707679779120799">This is another of Casey's books for school this next year. I loved it! I wish I could be a fly in the wall to hear the discussions they will have in class about the book. It is the story of Jacob who is a Jew, is sold as a slave after the massacres of Chmielnicki in the 17th Century. Jacob's wife and children and most of his family were murdered. He is kept as a slave for five years on a mountainside in Poland. Here he falls in love with Wanda, his owner's daughter, who is a widow herself. And,</span>
  <span id="freeText4888707679779120799" style="display:none">This is another of Casey's books for school this next year. I loved it! I wish I could be a fly in the wall to hear the discussions they will have in class about the book. It is the story of Jacob who is a Jew, is sold as a slave after the massacres of Chmielnicki in the 17th Century. Jacob's wife and children and most of his family were murdered. He is kept as a slave for five years on a mountainside in Poland. Here he falls in love with Wanda, his owner's daughter, who is a widow herself. And, he tries to continue in his beloved faith the best he can remember it. He is ransomed and freed and returned to his home town. However, he finds that his years as a slave have changed him forever. He misses Wanda so much, he returns to find her. They run away together which begins another kind of slavery. It is against the law to convert a Gentile to be a Jew, and it is also against the law for a Jew to hide as a Gentile, both being punishable by death. They love each other so much they choose to live as Jews. However, Wanda has to become the "deaf, mute" Sarah so as to not betray their deception. In doing so, the people think she is deaf so they do not hide their real feelings for their neighbors and "friends". I find their observations so appropriate for here and now. Some of my favorite quotes:<br /><br />"Yes, men and women who would rather have died than break the smallest of these ritualistic laws, slandered and gossiped openly, and treated the poor with contempt. Scholars lorded it over the ignorant; the elders divided privileges and preferments among themselves and their relatives and exploited the people generally . . . But, when Jacob entered the study house he met them all: the angry, the haughty, the obsequious, the crooked. They prayed and schemed, erected tall towers of legalism while they broke God's commandments". Pg 118<br /><br />"Piety was the cloak for envy and avarice. The Jews had learned nothing from their ordeal; rather suffering had pushed them lower." Pg. 119<br /><br />"The years of slavery had estranged him from life; he looked healthy, but was shattered within. He kept rummaging in the cabala and leafing through books of philosophy. Sometimes he was overwhelmed by the desire to flee, but he didn't know where. He doubted everything, with, as the saying goes, the kind of doubt which 'the heart does not share with the lips.'" Pg. 119<br /><br />"Despite the thirty-odd years he had lived in the world, Jacob was continually astonished at how many Jews obeyed only one half of the Torah. The very same people, who strictly observed the minor rituals and customs which were not even rooted in the Talmud, broke without thinking twice the most sacred laws, even the Ten Commandments. They wanted to be kind to God and not to man: but what did God need of man and his favors? What does a father want from his children but that they should not do injustice to each other?" Pg 219<br /><br /> <br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="4888707679779120799" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer4976670228596101783">Following the Chmielnicki massacres of the seventeenth century, A religious Jew becomes a slave in a Polish mountain village. He tries to live in this strange environment, withstanding temptations, keeping the Jewish religion customs. The villagers consider him strange and want him to become a Christian. When he refuses again and again, they aim to harm him.<br/><br/>This book has everything in it…<br/>A shocking description of the life of the Jews in Poland at that time and the atrocities that they suffer fro</span>
  <span id="freeText4976670228596101783" style="display:none">Following the Chmielnicki massacres of the seventeenth century, A religious Jew becomes a slave in a Polish mountain village. He tries to live in this strange environment, withstanding temptations, keeping the Jewish religion customs. The villagers consider him strange and want him to become a Christian. When he refuses again and again, they aim to harm him.<br /><br />This book has everything in it…<br />A shocking description of the life of the Jews in Poland at that time and the atrocities that they suffer from.<br />A picture of the Jewish customs, life, philosophy and similar.<br />Many references and comparison to the Bible and characters in the Bible.<br />A love story.<br /><br />One of the main themes in the book is criticism of the double standards of the Jewish community. <br />On one hand they are very pious in relation to the religious rules that concern the relationship between man and God. They invent new constraints and make a big fuss over any case of breaking these constraints.<br />On the other hand, the leaders (both civilian and religious) are corrupt, they cheat and steal and lie . They get rich from exploiting the poor people and hardly do anything to support the victims of the massacres.<br /><br />My feeling (and it is just my feeling) concerning this criticisms is that some of it(the exaggeration of observing all the religious rules that are between the believer and God) is aimed at the orthodox religious Jews of the time the book was written, and the seventeenth century story is just a cover.<br /></span>

<div id="review_65351066" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/65351066" />
    <a title="Kathy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1609554-kathy"><img alt="Kathy" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/65351066?book_show_action=true">Jul 28, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kathy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kathy" href="/user/show/1609554-kathy">Kathy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer65351066" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13840712505357520945">Once again Isaac Bashevis Singer' storytelling has arrested and spellbound me. I found this an intensely beautiful book. <br/><br/>Four years after the Chmielnicki massacres of the seventeenth century, Jacob, a Jewish slave and cowherd in a Polish village high in the mountains, falls in love with Wanda, his gentile master's daughter. Jacob is ransomed, and freed from his years of enforced slavery only to find it succeeded by another sort of slavery that would last as long as he lived. He is stunned by the</span>
  <span id="freeText13840712505357520945" style="display:none">Once again Isaac Bashevis Singer' storytelling has arrested and spellbound me. I found this an intensely beautiful book. <br /><br />Four years after the Chmielnicki massacres of the seventeenth century, Jacob, a Jewish slave and cowherd in a Polish village high in the mountains, falls in love with Wanda, his gentile master's daughter. Jacob is ransomed, and freed from his years of enforced slavery only to find it succeeded by another sort of slavery that would last as long as he lived. He is stunned by the intensity of his passion and love for Wanda, and how it changes everything that he thought he knew about himself. He and Wanda escape to a distant Jewish community, and build a life while concealing her identity. Jacob is racked with guilt not only for the sin of marrying a gentile, but also for his life of ongoing deceptions in the name of love. <br /><br />Jacob struggles with his religious beliefs, and recognizes that "We are all slaves. God's slaves." He admits free will, but also acknowledges powers stronger than himself. Enslaved by his religion and the expectations that others have of how that religion should be observed and practiced, he eventually comes to clearly understand that man's obligations toward God are easy to perform, the rules simple. However, he realizes that the essence of religion is the relation between man and his fellows. As is typical in life and the affairs of men, the relationships in this book are complex and compelling.<br /><br />"A voice within (Jacob) called out constantly for Wanda." <br />"The truth was that since childhood (Wanda) had been expecting and longing for (Jacob)."<br />I was deeply touched by the power of Jacob and Wanda's profound love. I won't soon forget the emotions that Isaac Bashevis Singer stirred within me as I read "The Slave".</span>
  <a data-text-id="13840712505357520945" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1157721905" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1157721905" />
    <a title="Jo Tsamaidis" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/34089194-jo-tsamaidis"><img alt="Jo Tsamaidis" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1598647459p2/34089194.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1157721905?book_show_action=true">Jan 05, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jo Tsamaidis" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jo Tsamaidis" href="/user/show/34089194-jo-tsamaidis">Jo Tsamaidis</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/34089194-jo-tsamaidis?shelf=my-five-star-faves">my-five-star-faves</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1157721905" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10773770190377243737">Beautifully written and absorbing novel. It is so rich in detail and yet nothing is superfluous; it is a story that deals with the real fundamentals of life -faith, hope and love. <br/><br/>Jacob is committed to God. Even as a slave, he tries to observe all of the Jewish laws and customs. He is committed to his eternal life despite all manner of distractions: brutal living conditions, pagan influence and sexual desire. I was struck by the contrast between the pagans who were also considered Gentiles and t</span>
  <span id="freeText10773770190377243737" style="display:none">Beautifully written and absorbing novel. It is so rich in detail and yet nothing is superfluous; it is a story that deals with the real fundamentals of life -faith, hope and love. <br /><br />Jacob is committed to God. Even as a slave, he tries to observe all of the Jewish laws and customs. He is committed to his eternal life despite all manner of distractions: brutal living conditions, pagan influence and sexual desire. I was struck by the contrast between the pagans who were also considered Gentiles and this Jew. The discipline of the Jew in sharp contrast to the pagans who live to survive and satisfy their animal instincts caused me to reflect upon the divine purpose of a chosen people. What could compel a people to look past their own animal instincts, if not God? And yet this story offers a deeper exploration of the Jewish faith in relation to the law. It shines a light on Jews who claim their inheritance by birthright by following the letter of the law but routinely violating the spirit of it. Jacob sees it and it repulses him in the same way as the Bolshevik atrocities (hard to stomach at times) and the undisciplined animal ways of the Gentiles. <br /><br />It is a story of hope. Jacob never loses hope in God. There is a point when Wanda becomes more than just an object of desire. Even Jacob seems to realize the Divine Hand at work, especially in returning to Wanda. I think this Divine Hand was in spite of the sorcery employed by Wanda. The theme of hope was especially strong towards the end of the story, when Jacob must go on alone. Can't say much more without giving away too much but hope is definitely present. At least twice characters note "those who want to die never do." Even these statements reminded me of the biblical idea that death is still the enemy. <br /><br />And love. The story is indeed a beautiful love story. For me, the beauty lies in the complexity of their love. It was a sacrificial love. The sacrifice may appear to be one sided with Wanda leaving behind so much of her historical self to unite with Jacob however given Jacob's exalted role within his community, he also sacrificed. I loved the whole transcendence of this love story. It has a sort of Divine order set against the particular brutal time of 17th century Poland. I am not sure that I have sufficient knowledge of the Jewish faith to appreciate the conversion and the ramifications of the comment " Jewish soul". However I think the book explores the divine nature of love in a most subtle and satisfying way. <br /><br />I think I could read this several times over and find new wisdom every time. </span>
  <a data-text-id="10773770190377243737" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <a title="Marc Gerstein" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Marc Gerstein" href="/user/show/15102294-marc-gerstein">Marc Gerstein</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer8899953680093843384">This is a fascinating and nuanced work by a Nobel Prize winning 20th century writer of Jewish-themed novels who reaches for a big-picture historical/theological/philosophical perspective. <br/><br/>On the surface, this is the story of a doomed love between a Jew who is captured and sold into slavery following at 16th Century pogrom and falls in love the with daughter of his master. But Romeo and Juliet, this isn’t. The situation faced by the Jewish community in Europe in the centuries following the post-R</span>
  <span id="freeText8899953680093843384" style="display:none">This is a fascinating and nuanced work by a Nobel Prize winning 20th century writer of Jewish-themed novels who reaches for a big-picture historical/theological/philosophical perspective. <br /><br />On the surface, this is the story of a doomed love between a Jew who is captured and sold into slavery following at 16th Century pogrom and falls in love the with daughter of his master. But Romeo and Juliet, this isn’t. The situation faced by the Jewish community in Europe in the centuries following the post-Roman era dispersion is front and center and as one who knows anything about history might expect, it’s dismal and dangerous. What’s really noteworthy here is that as bad as the Jews in this novel have it, Poland, the place where it’s set, was actually one of the better places for Jews to live back then (which says something about how bad other places were).<br /><br />But it wasn’t simply Jews/Good-Gentiles/Bad. The novel is much more challenging than that and really makes you work it you want to set up a good guys versus bad guys ledger. There are Jews and Gentiles in both columns.<br /><br />The only thing that disappointed me was the last quarter of the book. Once the major even toward which the novel was building occurred (I won’t mention it to avoid spoilers), all of the nuance and grabbed hold of me up till then vanished as Singer switched gears, threw subtlety out the window, and raced toward a romantic fairy tale ending.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8899953680093843384" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_1491348886" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1491348886" />
    <a title="Vishvapani" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/12392202-vishvapani"><img alt="Vishvapani" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1346400284p2/12392202.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1491348886?book_show_action=true">Jan 02, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Vishvapani" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Vishvapani" href="/user/show/12392202-vishvapani">Vishvapani</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1491348886" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17755885268885873395">The Slave is one of Bashevis Singer's strongest novels, and a good place to start if you have never read any of his books - if only because it is a short novel. It's also wonderfully well written, reflective and strongly felt. <br/>Several of Bashevis Singer's novels share a pattern: a man intends to be good but finds himself is drawn into love by his emotions. Choosing to be true to himself, he diverges from conventional Jewish virtue and encounters a new intensity of suffering that leads him more d</span>
  <span id="freeText17755885268885873395" style="display:none">The Slave is one of Bashevis Singer's strongest novels, and a good place to start if you have never read any of his books - if only because it is a short novel. It's also wonderfully well written, reflective and strongly felt. <br />Several of Bashevis Singer's novels share a pattern: a man intends to be good but finds himself is drawn into love by his emotions. Choosing to be true to himself, he diverges from conventional Jewish virtue and encounters a new intensity of suffering that leads him more deeply into life. He responds with a fresh turn towards righteousness and ends with a hard-won wisdom before he dies as some kind of a saint.<br />In his book on Shakespeare, Ted Hughes - venturing that every writer has only one fundamental story to tell - cites Bashevis Singer, suggesting that he found us story in writing Satan in Goray, has first published novel. The Slave is set in the same period: the aftermath of the Chmielnicki massacres of 1648, which devastated Polish Jewry and foreshadowed The Holocaust. Jacob the protagonist, is learned Jew who has been enslaved by Polish peasants, and falls in love with Wanda, a beautiful gentile. The book charts his journey as well as evoking the Jewish past. <br />The historical setting also allows Singer to explore the mental world that traditional Jews had inhabited, in which the Bible and Talmud were intertwined with daily life, and the universe was suffused by angels, dybbuks and a phantasmagoria of saints and miracles. That's what I find most alluring in the world this book creates: what was it like to inhabit such a universe, even as it collided with historical catastrophe and the moral failings of ordinary Jews? We usually think of Singer as a Jewish novelist, but this is also historical fiction and it shames most other novels in that genre with the depth of its imagining and the immediacy with which it shows how people think, how they feel and why they act as they do. </span>
  <a data-text-id="17755885268885873395" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1386305952" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1386305952" />
    <a title="Erez Davidi" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/43149722-erez-davidi"><img alt="Erez Davidi" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1575060449p2/43149722.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1386305952?book_show_action=true">Sep 08, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Erez Davidi" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Erez Davidi" href="/user/show/43149722-erez-davidi">Erez Davidi</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1386305952" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17678550606489502381">As a result of the Chmielnicki massacres in 1648, thousands of Jews lost their lives, among them was the family of the Jacob, who was taken as a slave later to be sold to Polish peasants. Up there, in the mountains, he is treated by everyone as subhuman, except by one Polish peasant - Wanda. Naturally, they fall in love.<br/><br/>After fellow Jews from his hometown pay the ransom and free Jacob, he tries to live a normal life in his hometown, yet he cannot forgot Wanda. So he decides to go to Poland and b</span>
  <span id="freeText17678550606489502381" style="display:none">As a result of the Chmielnicki massacres in 1648, thousands of Jews lost their lives, among them was the family of the Jacob, who was taken as a slave later to be sold to Polish peasants. Up there, in the mountains, he is treated by everyone as subhuman, except by one Polish peasant - Wanda. Naturally, they fall in love.<br /><br />After fellow Jews from his hometown pay the ransom and free Jacob, he tries to live a normal life in his hometown, yet he cannot forgot Wanda. So he decides to go to Poland and bring Wanda back with him. Since it was illegal at the time and punishable by death to convert a Christian to Judaism, he decides to pass her as a mute until she masters the Yiddish language, so her Polish accent won’t reveal her.<br /><br />Although thought to be a Jew, Wanda is mistreated by the community. Wanda, as a non-Jew living among Jews, is able to examine the Jewish community through a different set of eyes. Assumed to be mute, people feel free to talk in her presence. Fairly quickly, as Wanda herself is taught about Judaism by Jacob, she notices the hypocrisy, the decay and the lack of religious discipline within her community.<br /><br />Before Wanda dies in childbirth, she reveals the secret of her Christian birth. This leads to Jacob running away in fear of his life, leaving his son to be taken care of by people in the community. To cut a story short, Jacob returns to take his son to Israel.<br /><br />This is a very powerful story about a relationship between two people from different cultures and religions at a time when there was no tolerance for the other. It also a deep examination of the good and bad of religion.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17678550606489502381" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2859266849" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2859266849" />
    <a title="S Moss" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/33755471-s-moss"><img alt="S Moss" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2859266849?book_show_action=true">Jul 07, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="S Moss" class="user" itemprop="url" name="S Moss" href="/user/show/33755471-s-moss">S Moss</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback " href="/book/show/23340964-the-slave">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2859266849" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6521318349847450317">Better as Short Stories<br/><br/>Everything Singer wrote is creatively interesting with the added depth of historical context, but these components are unable to “salvage” this novel, which is repetitive, includes too many anecdotes and minor details about Jewish and Polish peasant life that distract from the main story, and becomes boring as Joseph constantly torments himself how to justify his passion for a gentile woman. The intriguing details about Polish peasant superstitions and licentious behavior </span>
  <span id="freeText6521318349847450317" style="display:none">Better as Short Stories<br /><br />Everything Singer wrote is creatively interesting with the added depth of historical context, but these components are unable to “salvage” this novel, which is repetitive, includes too many anecdotes and minor details about Jewish and Polish peasant life that distract from the main story, and becomes boring as Joseph constantly torments himself how to justify his passion for a gentile woman. The intriguing details about Polish peasant superstitions and licentious behavior seem anthropological, as do Singer’s factoids about life in small Jewish villages constantly being attacked by Cossacks and warring European nations. Now that such cultures and behaviors have vanished, his detailed descriptions of peasants’ often savage and even pagan behavior provide an interesting glimpse into the dark, cruel life Jacob lived as a slave of these peasants.<br /><br />While the novel may be valuable to students of folklore, pagan peasant life, Jewish history, and even theological issues, as Jacob cites numerous Jewish texts to examine his situation and help him decide what he can do and whether this would be acceptable, his unique situation, where he’s pursued by and loves a Polish peasant girl, is outside both Polish and Jewish laws and cultural norms. Thus it is inherently unlikely, except that Jacob’s return from slavery is during the period of messianic fervor surrounding Sabbati Zevi, who used obscure cabbalistic writings to support his ideas that upended traditional Jewish religious, social and sexual norms. Singer hints at this when he says Jacob’s “investigations of the cabala since his return [from slavery] had uncovered the doctrine that all lust was of divine origin…Coupling was the universal act underlying everything…God’s holy names themselves were mysterious unions of the male and female principles” (111). Only at the novel’s end does Singer reveal that Jacob and his equally devout son for a time had belonged to Zevi’s sect. For more background on this interesting movement, Singer’s fascinating novel Satan in Goray (1933) is a detailed, compelling description of this exceptional period in the 1650s and 60s when a Jewish peasant mania occurred because Zevi convinced many that he was the Messiah and the End of Days was about to happen, after which Jews would magically fly to Israel and no longer suffer persecutions, such as Chmielnicki had caused in 1648.<br /><br />Between 1648 and 1655 as many as 100,000 Jews were tortured, massacred and their property confiscated or destroyed, and Jacob frequently hears about specific atrocities. The Chmielnicki massacre occurs at the beginning of this novel, after which Jacob is abducted by bandits who sell him to the Poles, and he enter slavery for six years. He is ransomed and returns to his Jewish village; after several years (during which Zevi mania was spreading) he goes in search of the faithfully waiting Wanda, who easily agrees to go with him. One of the signs of the End of Days was that among the mystical happenings many Christians converted to Judaism, either out of conviction or because they too wanted to escape miserable poverty and fly to Israel. Thus marriages of gentiles and Jews would signify the Redemption was at hand. “Entire communities of Jews turned Christian and though some later resumed their own faith, others remained in darkness” (122). His conversion of Sarah he thinks of as having “saved a soul from idolatry” (132), and when he says that now she is completely separated from her family, and Jacob is her only relative, she demands that her beloved father also be taken into paradise or she won’t go (133). Perhaps this background explains why Wanda agrees to convert when the penalty was being burned alive, and why Jacob would think a converted gentile might be accepted by his Jewish community. By the time she dies in childbirth, he is convinced that his marriage and her “conversion” are in violation of all laws, and his sin has resulted in her murder, although later as a Zevi devotee his attitude changes. Such reversals occur throughout, and Singer never explores why she would agree to marry Jacob except that she had seen him in a vision and had a mystical conviction she is fated to be joined to him, which, coupled to her passionate love, provides a sentimentally satisfying explanations. <br /><br />There are many redundant, obscure sections where Jacob agonizes over his situation, torn between his passion and lust, which Jewish tradition considers are sinfully caused by Satan, and his obsession with following all Jewish rules and precepts. He constantly questions whether a true conversion can be based upon love, which is the only factor that can motivate a woman (only men can make a religious decision), and examines exceptional situations, many of which come from cabbalistic writings, as possible models for his behavior. The beginning of the final section “The Return” goes into limited detail about Zevi’s cabbalistic ideas, which caused some to cease “to follow the Torah, believing the law would be annulled with the coming of the Messiah. Still others ferreted out hints in the Bible that to be utterly evil was the way to redemption, and they indulged in every variety of abomination” (232). After Sarah’s death and Jacob’s migration to Israel, during the period of Messianic fervor their union came to symbolize the “physical action” that Zevi encouraged, and their intermarriage fulfilled texts that suggested “Jacob and Sarah as precursors of the Redemption” (239); for a time her grave was treated as a holy site by local women, until Zevi’s conversion to Islam (1666), when his followers were scorned and excommunicated. <br /><br />These ideas and texts are unknown to the average reader, so most of this analysis is of little general interest and probably more confusing and problematic than useful for the narrative. The same could be said of Jacob’s tormented analysis of the Torah and other Jewish texts to find explanations that would justify converting and marrying Wanda, as well as provide answers to basic questions why evil is permitted, what is the nature of God, and is there eternity, most of which are unanswerable and some border on blasphemy for the devout, who are encouraged not to question. The reason for Jacob’s manic focus on such issues is that he was always an aloof, quiet misfit. who refused charity and took on hard physical labor, atypical for any Jew aspiring to scholarly status, although Jacob always carefully avoided this identification despite his intense love of books. He “had his own ways of thinking and acting,… interpreted the Torah in his own manner, was accustomed to suspicion and mockery. Even as a child he had been a misfit” (241). Because Singer places this information at the end of the novel, it is barely interesting and makes Jacob seem a different person from the man in the first two parts. In the end, the novel seems like a fanciful description of an ideal marriage that units passion to devotion, combines some gentile common sense with obedience to important Jewish principles, ended by a brief declaration of why Jacob acted as he did that may explain his idiosyncratic behavior. <br /><br />Because Jacob is determined not to reject his heritage (one of the novel’s leitmotifs), even though his looks and speech would enable him easily to pass as gentile (only his circumcision would mark him as Jewish), in the end Wanda must convert, which involves not only endless study of Jewish religious rules but also having to play “dumb” because she can never speak Yiddish without being identified as gentile. Jacob asks her to make a much greater sacrifice than he makes in marrying her. After the ridicule she suffers from her Jewish neighbors, who criticize and mock her “handicap,” her horrible death in childbirth, and unsanctified burial, his shame and remorse for asking her to make such sacrifices may be the reasons he takes his infant son and follows a disciple of Zevi to Israel, where a new approach to Judaism seemed to be flourishing that was free of the restrictions, intolerance and impiety he had always experienced. Singer implies rigid traditions allowed many who weren’t truly pious and faithful to the spirit of Judaism to pass as pious because they devoted their attention to the laws’ minutiae. “His years away from the Torah had changed his views. Now conscious of much he had not realized, he saw that one law in the Torah generated a dozen in the Mishnah, and five dozen in the Gemara…Each generation added its own strictures…A new thought occurred to him: if this continued, nothing would be kosher” (99). Jacob and Sarah frequently wonder how Jews can consider themselves pious and yet behave cruelly to fellow Jews; Jacob often mentions this hypocrisy when he describes people’s hostile, exclusionary attitude toward him and Sarah, as well as toward poor people generally. His quandary is underlined when he’s teaching the converted Sarah about Jewish laws: “The sacred books warn that all those who gossip, ridicule, or speak evil of others, will burn in the fires of Gehenna.” She says Jewish women frequently break these laws and yet refuse to eat pork, so these must be lesser sins. All Jacob can reply is, “That’s the way it’s always been…It’s easier not to eat pig than to curb your tongue” (135). <br /><br />Strict Jewish laws, difficult to challenge because of their traditional, religious weight, at first make it impossible for the devout Jacob to accept his passionate nature. Instead of freely accepting Wanda’s complete love, he constantly critiques her peasant circumstances, although acknowledging she’s much above the peasant norm. “The peasants here had not even risen to the level of the Christians. They still followed the customs of the ancient pagans” (12), which are described in lurid detail. Jewish family and community practices, established by religious texts, are presented as superior to both. At points Jacob seems almost arrogant when he cannot make any attempt to join with Wanda’s family in celebrations because the food isn’t kosher, or there are other activities such as dancing and singing that are forbidden. While many of the peasants are depicted as truly offensive and evil, Jacob’s stern rejection of even simple gentile pleasures seems excessively rigid. Only once does he feel pity for “a monstrous, square-headed girl with a goiter on her neck…compassion for this creature…deformed and misshapen, a mooncalf, swept over Jacob. Who could tell…what sinful soul had been incarcerated in the girl’s body. Hers was not an ordinary cry but the wail of a spirit who has gazed into the abyss and seen a torment from which there is no escape. Through some miracle this animal comprehended its own bestiality and mourned its lot” (53). In the end he’s fearful what the girl might do to him, so he avoids her. Although at times the peasants ask Jacob to come and live with them, he scorns their offer as contamination, and unfortunately his firm rejection of contact only increases their hostility, even though Singer intends it to show Jacob’s religious faithfulness even under extreme circumstances.<br /><br />The novel’s major conflict is simple: how can two people, who love each other, be married when both their communities prohibit such a marriage upon penalty of death. Frequently Jacob goes over the catalog of Biblical Jewish men who had affairs or married gentiles. Even so, he isn’t confident God will excuse him or Sarah for their sin of marrying. This insoluble problem occupies him throughout the novel, and it becomes truly irritating to hear him trying to find excuses, then discarding them, but all the while acknowledging that he and Sarah are happy despite all the trials. While his undying struggle to justify his marriage probably appeals to Romantic Jewish readers, for many others these trials seem the result of outdated traditions, narrow cultural norms, and societal constraints that for the most part have thankfully vanished. Perhaps this is why Singer is attracted to the period of Sabbati Zevi, when precisely such narrow, rigid Jewish traditions were challenged and sometimes discarded. However, the movement resulted in abuses and ultimately achieved nothing, although for a brief period it provided Jews different options for their religious practices. <br /><br />Since Wanda is unconcerned about giving up Christianity and says from the first that she’s eager to convert, her problem is easily resolved. Jacob, however, splits another hair and worries that her desire for conversion is the result of her love for him and not her devout decision directed by God. In addition, she knows neither Yiddish (the language spoken by the Jews) nor anything about their religious rituals and traditional customs, which are complex and control every aspect of their lives. If Singer hadn’t pointed out that the Torah promulgates codes of behavior that are more “advanced” in terms of community organization, sanitation and food handling than those practiced at the time by pagan peasants or even the average Christians, the contemporary reader might think Wanda’s decision to adopt Judaism would result in more restrictions than advantages. <br /> <br />When Jacob is enslaved, he lives in the mountains where his master, Wanda’s father, sends him to tend his cows during the summer, and despite his enslavement, he finds joy in simple, natural events, like a glorious sunrise or patterns of light in the trees or birdsong each morning. “A hawk, wings outstretched, glided tranquilly with a strange slowness beyond all earthly anxieties. It appeared to Jacob that the bird had been flying without interruptions since creation” (10). In a sense, this is an Eden, distant from brutal Polish life and the quarrelsome, restrictive Jewish existence. He doesn’t regret his stay on the mountains, except that he has none of his religious books and has a hard time following the Jewish calendar to celebrate holy days correctly, which ironically will immerse him in all the anxieties of Jewish life. On his later return to get Wanda, he notes that the fresh air and beauty of the countryside are a welcome contrast to the constraints in a Jewish community where he was shut up daily reading books. “How beautiful the countryside was…Doubt, dissension, discord dwelt within him, but the fields exuded harmony, tranquility, fruitfulness. The sky was blue, the weather warm with the mercy of summer… A hidden hand had shaped and modeled each stalk, blade of grass, leaf, worm” (104). It is mainly at moments when he can look at the stars in the night sky and see there almost a cabalistic text of God’s ultimate control and goodness that he is most at peace. “The summer night throbbed with joy; from all sides came music. Warm winds bore the smells of grain, fruit, and pine trees to him. Itself a cabalistic book, the night was crowded with sacred names and symbols--mystery upon mystery…The stars looked like letters of the alphabet, vowel points, notes of music..The world was a parchment scrawled with words and song…He was surrounded by powers, some good, some evil, some cruel, some merciful, but each with its own nature and its own task to perform…The struggle was going on without as well as within him” (163). In this sensual environment, Wanda is a great temptation, which he valiantly resists. It’s amusing that when he won’t take the first step, she proposes marriage to him, he rejects her proposal because of his religion, she offers to co-habit with him, which is standard practice among her peasant neighbors, and although Jacob is tempted by this idea, fornication is also forbidden by Jewish law. So he resists mightily, until the inevitable happens one rainy night. Then she willingly undergoes cleansing rituals in a frigid mountain stream as her first demonstration what she’s willing to do for him.<br /><br />Critiques of Jewish practices occur when Jacob is working for the debauched aristocrat, Adam Pilitzky, who quizzes him about Jewish behavior and beliefs, starting with Jacob’s refusal to drink wine with him because his religion forbids it. Singer gives many shocking details about the Pilitzkys’ sexual practices to show them as deviant, corrupt, incompetent, and in need of Jewish tenants to do the work on their lands, although some Jews employed by Pilitzky are no better ethically. While Pilitzky has some tolerance toward Jews who work on his land and create his wealth, he’s quite rigid that only Catholics are Christians, while Orthodox and Protestant don’t qualify. He questions Jacob sharply as to why Jews stubbornly refuse to eat foods or intermarry, believe they are a chosen people, yet are impoverished and constantly suffering. Christians “suffer for a reason. Our souls are purified through what we endure and rise to heaven” (154). Adam recounts that he heard ecstatic singing in the synagogue, but later Jews crushed each other to death when the Cossacks arrived. “Where was the God you praise and beseech, whose dear children you claim to be? How do you deal with these facts, Jew? How can you sleep at night remembering” (154). Adam’s wife says that Christians too have no answer to this. “Even searching for an answer is blasphemous” (154), and Adam grasps at Democritus’ idea that “chance ruled everything…The world…was the result of blind powers” (155). Ironically, he obsessively hopes to see a miracle to convince him there is a God, which would give him a reason to live. <br /><br />After Sarah dies in an appalling, naturalistically described childbirth that goes on for many pages, Jacob meets one of Zevi’s apostles, who are wandering around trying to convert people. Perhaps Jacob’s attraction to Zevi is due to feeling lonely and exiled from all Jews, as well as overwhelmed with sorrow by Sarah’s death. In any case, Jacob’s life in Pilitz ends with him taking his infant son and leaving for Israel. Between her death and the third section, twenty years pass during which Jacob has become one of Zevi’s followers and later rejected him as a false prophet when he converted to Islam rather than be killed for his Jewish faith; none of this is clearly described. Jacob has returned to Pilitz looking for Sarah’s bones to take back to Israel, where he and his son have established happy, respected lives. The novel’s third section “The Return” is the most confusing because Singer suddenly describes how Jacob has a unique personality and chooses his own way rejecting the ways of others, which has made him an oddball, but one who is considered very devout and maybe one of the thirty-six honorable men who sustain the world’s just functioning. Such details at this point in his life is a bizarre way to conclude the novel, especially since his life as a follower of Zevi and afterwards is never described. Whether this section is intended to justify his “conversion” of Wanda, or explain how his sorrow and regret led him to follow Zevi, is never clear. Instead Singer describes that when Jacob returns for Sarah’s bones, he refuses offers of hospitality and stays in the poorhouse, where its uncleanliness means he can’t perform his prayers; overnight he becomes ill and quickly dies. <br /><br />Although the novel is based on a very romantic idea with two likeable, well-developed main characters, and sections of beautiful natural description, it is overwhelmed by factual details, repetitive theological arguments, anecdotes about Polish peasant life and Jewish traditions and experiences. It lacks a firm structure and editorial sense how many anecdotes about Jewish demons and Polish evil spirits a novel needs. For readers seeking a glimpse into pagan and Jewish peasant culture, there are interesting stories, but for a well-organized, enthralling novel on Zevi’s movement, a better choice is Singer’s Satan in Goray. Superb short stories about pre-World War II European Jewish life are in The Spinoza of Market Street, which contains many beautifully developed incidents and episodes similar to those in this novel.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6521318349847450317" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer9421769826458676513">Theme: Continue to be Jewish after the Holocaust. <br/>The Slave is really about the Holocaust. The question that is raised is: Does God really exist? And is it worthwhile to stay Jewish?<br/>Jews didn't fight back, they were not violent, they were spiritual. <br/>It's a parallel history like The Crucible (Arthur Miller).   <span id="freeText9421769826458676513" style="display:none">Theme: Continue to be Jewish after the Holocaust. <br />The Slave is really about the Holocaust. The question that is raised is: Does God really exist? And is it worthwhile to stay Jewish?<br />Jews didn't fight back, they were not violent, they were spiritual. <br />It's a parallel history like The Crucible (Arthur Miller). Miller was afraid of being punished (McCarthyism). For Singer it would have been too painful to write about the Holocaust. He had lost his family and he could only deal with that indirectly. <br />Other theme: Understanding Judaism. At first Jacob's knowledge is formalistic, later less formal, more spiritual, less bound by the letter of the law and more about the spirit behind the law. <br />He sleeps with Wanda, there's a spiritual base for this. The novel redefines Judaism and endorses Jacob's new interpretation of Judaism. <br />In the end he has become a saint, a holy man. And Wanda is now in the Jewish cemetry, God has included her. <br />Singer admires the Jews for still being Jewish. In spite of all their suffering they are still loyal. It would have been easier to become Christians or atheists. So the novel is an endorsement of the continuation of the Jewish identity. <br />Towards the middle of the novel Jabcon begins to doubt God. In the last part he learns to accept. He accepts that we can't answer the question why all this happened. He is more religious at a higher level. <br />(This novel was part of the course: Jewish-American Literature in the Post-War Period. Dr. Paul Gabriner, Amsterdam University 2005)</span>

  <span id="freeText110184474283805695" style="display:none">Ok, so this is MY opinion on The Slave, so pardon me if you did not like it. I was waiting impatiently to finish this book, I honestly don't know why exactly, but because I have a rule of thumb to finish the book whether it's good or bad if I pass 30 pages, I had to complete the novel. The plot is good, and, in fact, one that is new to me. However, Singer talked so much about Judaism to the point that I didn't understand a couple of pages because he included so many Jewish terms without even explaining them. He really shows the love Wanda had for Jacob in a very nice way. I mean, Wanda literally sacrificed so much to marry him (I honestly don't know why). He also loved her so much that he committed several sins for her sake (he was a very very very religious Jewish guy). <br />Anyways, the book was ok.</span>

  <td colspan="2">
    <div class="updateActionLinks">
